                <div class="date-outer">
                 <h2 class="date-header">
                  <span>
                   Wednesday, May 6, 2015
                  </span>
                 </h2>
                 <div class="date-posts">
                  <div class="post-outer">
                   <div class="post hentry">
                    <a name="1268085026970319883">
                    </a>
                    <h3 class="post-title entry-title">
                     <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2008/03/which-lens-should-i-buy.html">
                      Which lens should I buy for my DSLR?
                     </a>
                    </h3>
                    <div class="post-header">
                     <div class="post-header-line-1">
                     </div>
                    </div>
                    <div class="post-body entry-content">
                     <p>
                      <strong>
                       <font color="#ff8000">
                        Major Update
                       </font>
                      </strong>
                      : May, 6 2015
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      After
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2012/09/what-camera-should-i-buy-2012-edition.html" target="_blank">
                       which camera should I buy
                      </a>
                      , the 2nd most common question I hear from people is which lens should I buy?
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      <strong>
                       NOTE
                      </strong>
                      : This article covers
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_single-lens_reflex_camera" target="_blank">
                       DSLR
                      </a>
                      s. In the future I hope to work on a similar article for
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mirrorless_interchangeable-lens_camera" target="_blank">
                       mirrorless
                      </a>
                      cameras, but this article does not cover that topic in any way.
                      <br/>
                      <br/>
                      No matter which platform you choose, you should remember that if you take photography as a long term hobby that you will replace the camera body over time, but you probably will be able to use your lenses for many years with multiple camera bodies. This means that if you make wise lens choices that they will be good investments for many years to come. You should also understand that you can spend thousands of dollars on a camera body, but if you match it to a crappy lens, you are going to get a crappy picture. It's just like in the world of audio where
                      <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B000YPCJIM/ref=as_li_ss_tl?ie=UTF8&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=390957&amp;creativeASIN=B000YPCJIM&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;tag=ronrmarsblo-20" target="_blank">
                       a $4500+ receiver
                      </a>
                      matched to
                      <a href="http://www.amazon.com/gp/product/B001AVRD62?ie=UTF8&amp;tag=ronrmarsblo-20&amp;linkCode=as2&amp;camp=1789&amp;creative=390957&amp;creativeASIN=B001AVRD62" target="_blank">
                       $120 speakers
                      </a>
                      is going to give you $120 sound.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Your lens is what captures the light and sends the image data to the sensor, so quality is the utmost importance. When get into photography you should prepare yourself to spend more money on the lens (or lenses) than you spend on the camera body itself (if image quality is your top priority).
                      <br/>
                      <br/>
                      With this in mind, you also need to think how you are really going to be using your lens when making your first lens purchase. The reality is that most of us can only afford one or two lenses up front. We want something that is practical when we are walking around taking pictures on vacation, or when we are indoors taking pictures of our toddler. Since babies and toddlers don't stay still for pictures, you are also going to need to use a fast shutter speed which means you need a fast lens, so in that sense it is a lot like shooting sports. :-)
                      <br/>
                      <br/>
                      A
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fast_lens" target="_blank">
                       fast lens
                      </a>
                      in photography geek speak is usually one that has a aperture (F-stop number) of 2.8 or lower. Its called fast because you can generally use faster shutter speeds with these lenses as they get more light to the sensor. However, aperture also controls depth of field so the smaller the f-stop number the less depth of field (which can be desirable on long lenses and challenging on short ones). There are times where you dont want a super shallow
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Depth_of_field" target="_blank">
                       depth of field
                      </a>
                      (i.e., f/1.4 might get one of your toddlers eye in focus, but not the other  and its typically not the one you want because they move so much).
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      At f/2.8 you will generally get enough light into the camera in most situations to avoid using a flash. Often times theyll help you have a reasonable shutter speed to avoid motion blur of your subject (assuming they are a slow moving adult). The problem here is that, generally speaking (especially in the zoom lens world), f/2.8 lenses are expensive.
                      <br/>
                      <br/>
                      Your first lens should realistically be a zoom lens and you'll want to cover at least 24mm to 70mm range (or equivalent on your cropped sensor lens). If your budget can afford it then it should be a f/2.8 or a f/4. For Nikon this means the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/520637-USA/Nikon_2164_AF_S_Zoom_Nikkor_24_70mm.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       AF-S Zoom Nikkor 24-70mm f/2.8G ED
                      </a>
                      and for Canon it means the
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2012/09/comparison-canon-24-70-f28l-ii-vs-24.html" target="_blank">
                       24-70mm f/2.8 II USM
                      </a>
                      . For the best results, youll want
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Image_stabilization">
                       Image Stabilization
                      </a>
                      (or Vibration Reduction in Nikon speak, or OS is also used but several third parties) but not all fast lenses feature this technology so sometimes you have to make a tradeoff. When all things are equal, I generally recommend getting a stabilized lens. This means that the
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2013/02/review-canon-24-70mm-f4l-is-vs-24-70.html">
                       24-70 f/4L IS
                      </a>
                      might actually serve most people better thanks to its stabilization support, even if its only a f/4 lens.
                     </p>
                     <h4>
                      <strong>
                       Before you buy, consider renting -
                      </strong>
                      with a discount!
                     </h4>
                     <p>
                      Can afford the latest hot new lens? Me either  ha, ha! Its still fun and worth checking out and building a wish list. You can also rent your favorite lens have your day of fun with it too!
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      My friends at
                      <a href="http://ronmartblog.com/2009/11/review-lensrentalscom-lens-rental.html">
                       LensRentals.com
                      </a>
                      offer discounts on my
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/p/discount-codes.html">
                       discount coupon code page
                      </a>
                      where you can rent your favorite lens, so be sure to consider that option if purchasing is too expensive for you.
                     </p>
                     <p align="center">
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/p/discount-codes.html" target="_blank">
                       <img alt="5% Discount on your rental order" border="0" src="http://ronmart.smugmug.com/Blog/Reviews/LRlogo/1180492785_Xjv2J-S.png"/>
                      </a>
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Lets face it, lenses are expensive! Nothing is worse than finally pulling the trigger and having a lens sit around and collect dust because you realize that you made a mistake. After all, some lenses are very specialized so while they are fun  there is such a thing as too much of a good thing! Ive found myself in this shape with big sports lenses, fisheyes and tilt-shift lenses where I just didnt use them enough to justify the cost.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Renting lenses is a great way to find out if youll really like a lens enough to own it or if youll use it so infrequently that renting as needed makes sense. By renting, I quickly learned that big lenses like the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/732109-USA/Canon_4412B002_EF_400mm_f_2_8L_IS.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       400mm f/2.8L IS II USM
                      </a>
                      are awesome when you need them, they are a pain to have around the house when you dont! Whats more, they are a big investment, so even if I rented it for 30 four day rentals, I still wouldnt have spent as much as it cost to own that lens! Since I have a photography business, that rental is tax deductible too!
                     </p>
                     <h4>
                      <strong>
                       With so many choices, how do I know if a lens is right for me?
                      </strong>
                     </h4>
                     <p>
                      One of the easiest ways to understand which lens is the right lens for you is to ask the right question. All too often I hear people saying, "what should I get a 50mm f/1.4 or a 70-200mm f/2.8?" In life there are few stupid questions, but that my friend is a stupid question. Why? Because a 50mm prime has an entirely different purpose than a 70-200mm f/2.8 zoom lens, and generally you don't get either or you get both (eventually). Instead, you should ask yourself what do you plan to shoot?
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Another reality is that there is no one size fits all lens. While many people might think that a super zoom like the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/319784-USA/Canon_9322A002_28_300mm_f_3_5_5_6L_IS_USM.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Canon EF 28-300mm f/3.5-5.6L IS USM
                      </a>
                      or
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/872407-REG/Nikon_AF_S_DX_18_300mm_f_3_5_5_6G.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Nikon AF-S DX NIKKOR 18-300mm f/3.5-5.6G ED VR
                      </a>
                      come close, my real world testing of both has left me underwhelmed. Whats more, when you zoom these out to 300mm youll super need fast shutter speeds (1/300 sec+) at their maximum focal lengths, yet youll be shooting them at their minimum
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aperture" target="_blank">
                       aperture
                      </a>
                      of f/5.6. This means youll be using high ISOs for anything that moves on a sunny day and a cloudy day or indoor shot will mean youll most likely have to shoot your maximum ISO. High ISOs mean
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2009/03/noise-reduction-roundup-1-of-5.html" target="_blank">
                       noisy photos
                      </a>
                      , so youll lose detail on what area already soft lenses so the end result will often disappoint. Yes, its true there are some great photographers like
                      <a href="http://ronmartblog.com/2009/08/top-photographers-interview-bryan.html" target="_blank" title="Bryan Peterson">
                       Bryan Peterson
                      </a>
                      out there who can do amazing things with one of these lenses, but ask yourself  are you as good as Bryan? If the answer is no, then put the odds forever in your favour by getting a better lens!
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Instead of buying the one size fits all lens that screams I dont know what Im doing, you should pick a category that makes sense for what you will shoot the most and then purchase the best that your budget allows. To help you with this I'll offer some categories with some recommended lenses, and you can choose the order in which you should tackle the categories:
                     </p>
                     <h4>
                      Cropped vs Full Frame
                     </h4>
                     <p>
                      When shopping for lenses, remember that most of the more inexpensive cameras will have a smaller sensor called a cropped sensor. If you have a cropped sensor camera then youll have the option of purchasing cropped sensor lenses which are typically cheaper than their full-frame counterparts.In the Canon world lenses for cropped sensors are called EF-S lenses in the Nikon world you will be looking DX-Format lenses. The good news is that all cropped sensor cameras will work with the full frame (35mm film equivalent) sensor lenses which are called EF lenses for Canon and FX-Format for Nikon. However, your full-frame cameras can NOT use cropped sensor lenses so be sure to pay attention when shopping.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      <font color="#ff0000">
                       <strong>
                        WARNING
                       </strong>
                      </font>
                      : Certain Nikon bodies require AF-S lenses due to their lack of a AF drive motor, so buyer beware when lens shopping. Be sure to consult your manual for details on what lenses are compatible with your camera.
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      All Purpose
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      This is typically going to be the lens on your camera the most for family vacations, your kids birthday party, or when you arent sure what youll need.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      <em>
                       <font color="#ff8000">
                        <strong>
                         <em>
                          I recommend that everyone own one of these as their first lens
                         </em>
                        </strong>
                       </font>
                      </em>
                      instead of the kit lens if you can afford it. Now the best kit lens Ive ever tested was the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/872406-REG/Nikon_AF_S_24_85mm_f_3_5_4_5G_ED.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Nikon AF-S NIKKOR 24-85mm f/3.5-4.5G ED VR
                      </a>
                      , so Nikon shooters can compromise there and still have a very good lens. Canon doesnt have an equivalent great kit lens at this price point, but they have a broader selection of a great all purpose lenses. The 5D series is also offered with the excellent but aging
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/397662-USA/Canon_0344B002_24_105mm_f_4L_IS_USM.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       24-105mm f/4L IS USM
                      </a>
                      which I still own to this day. With that said, heres some great options that I recommend:
                     </p>
                     <p>
                     </p>
                     <table border="0" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0">
                      <tbody>
                       <tr>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <p align="center">
                          <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/843008-USA/Canon_5175B002_EF_24_70mm_f_2_8L_II.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                           <img border="0" height="250" src="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/images/largeimages/843008.jpg" style="border-left-width: 0px; border-right-width: 0px; border-bottom-width: 0px; border-top-width: 0px" width="250"/>
                          </a>
                          <br/>
                          Canon
                          <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/843008-USA/Canon_5175B002_EF_24_70mm_f_2_8L_II.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                           24-70mm f/2.8L II USM
                          </a>
                          (
                          <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2012/09/comparison-canon-24-70-f28l-ii-vs-24.html" target="_blank">
                           review
                          </a>
                          )
                         </p>
                        </td>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <img border="0" height="250" src="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/images/images345x345/520637.jpg" style="border-left-width: 0px; border-right-width: 0px; border-bottom-width: 0px; border-top-width: 0px" width="250"/>
                         <br/>
                         Nikon
                         <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/520637-USA/Nikon_2164_AF_S_Zoom_Nikkor_24_70mm.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                          AF-S 24-70mm f/2.8G ED
                         </a>
                        </td>
                       </tr>
                       <tr>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <img border="0" height="250" src="http://static.bhphoto.com/images/images200x200/898652.jpg" style="border-left-width: 0px; border-right-width: 0px; border-bottom-width: 0px; border-top-width: 0px" width="250"/>
                         <br/>
                         <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/898652-USA/Canon_6313b002_EF_24_70mm_f_4_0L_IS.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                          Canon EF 24-70mm f/4L IS USM Lens
                         </a>
                         <br/>
                         (
                         <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2013/02/review-canon-24-70mm-f4l-is-vs-24-70.html" target="_blank">
                          review
                         </a>
                         )
                        </td>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <br/>
                         <p align="center">
                          <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/872406-REG/Nikon_AF_S_24_85mm_f_3_5_4_5G_ED.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                           <img border="0" height="250" src="http://static.bhphoto.com/images/images500x500/872406.jpg" style="border-left-width: 0px; border-right-width: 0px; border-bottom-width: 0px; border-top-width: 0px" title="Copyright Ron Martinsen - ALL RIGHTS RESERVED" width="250"/>
                           <br/>
                           <em>
                            Nikon AF-S NIKKOR 24-85mm f/3.5-4.5G ED VR
                           </em>
                          </a>
                         </p>
                         <p>
                         </p>
                        </td>
                       </tr>
                      </tbody>
                     </table>
                     <p>
                      If you are shopping for Nikon then the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/872406-REG/Nikon_AF_S_24_85mm_f_3_5_4_5G_ED.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Nikon AF-S NIKKOR 24-85mm f/3.5-4.5G ED VR Lens
                      </a>
                      kit lens that I tested with the
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2012/10/review-nikon-d600my-favorite-nikon-ever.html" target="_blank">
                       D600
                      </a>
                      is one of the sharpest kit lenses Ive ever tested. In fact, when I reviewed the
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2013/11/review-nikon-d610-part-i-of-ii-real.html" target="_blank" title="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2013/11/review-nikon-d610-part-i-of-ii-real.html">
                       D610
                      </a>
                      I wished that I would have had it!
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Dont believe me? Go check out the blur index at SLRGear.com for the
                      <a href="http://slrgear.com/reviews/showproduct.php/product/1526/cat/13" target="_blank">
                       24-85 here
                      </a>
                      and the
                      <a href="http://slrgear.com/reviews/showproduct.php/product/1121/cat/13" target="_blank">
                       24-70 f/2.8 here
                      </a>
                      . As the charts show, you wouldnt want this lens for a
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2014/08/review-nikon-d810-with-zeiss-otus-55mm.html" target="_blank">
                       D810
                      </a>
                      , but its a great choice as a starter lens for the
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2015/04/review-nikon-d7200-18-140mm-lensthe.html" target="_blank">
                       D7200
                      </a>
                      and it served me well with the D600 (
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2012/10/review-nikon-d600my-favorite-nikon-ever.html" target="_blank">
                       see my review for sample pics
                      </a>
                      taken with it).
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      The popular Nikon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/729948-USA/Nikon_2193_AF_S_NIKKOR_24_120mm_f_4G.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       AF-S 24-120mm f/4G ED VR
                      </a>
                      (
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2011/01/b-hands-on-nikon-d7000-24-120mm-f4g-ed.html" target="_blank">
                       my review
                      </a>
                      ) isnt a bad lens, but its definitely soft by todays standards. Ive also been very unimpressed with the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/729950-USA/Nikon_2191_AF_S_NIKKOR_28_300mm_f_3_5_5_6G.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       28-300mm
                      </a>
                      and despite the buzz on the web, the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/644744-USA/Nikon_2192_AF_S_DX_NIKKOR_18_200mm.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       AF-S 18-200mm f/3.5-5.6G ED VR II
                      </a>
                      hasnt knocked my socks off either.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Despite a better lens selection, Canon has no bargain lens that is on par with the Nikon 24-85mm. The Canon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/397662-USA/Canon_0344B002_24_105mm_f_4L_IS_USM.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       24-105mm
                      </a>
                      is a great lens which I still own, but its definitely soft compared to the newer models
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2012/09/comparison-canon-24-70-f28l-ii-vs-24.html" target="_blank">
                       as you can see in this article
                      </a>
                      . Cropped sensor shooters often go for the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/425812-USA/Canon_1242B002AA_EF_S_17_55mm_f_2_8_IS.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       EF-S 17-55mm f/2.8 IS USM
                      </a>
                      , but Ive heard enough complaints about the build quality that Id still recommend people spend a bit more to get the outstanding
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/898652-USA/Canon_6313b002_EF_24_70mm_f_4_0L_IS.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Canon EF 24-70mm f/4L IS USM Lens
                      </a>
                      (
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2013/02/review-canon-24-70mm-f4l-is-vs-24-70.html" target="_blank">
                       my review
                      </a>
                      ). Yes, its a f/4 vs a f/2.8, but its also a sharper lens with better IS so real world shooting will favor the 24-70 under most conditions.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      The
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/buy/Sigma/Ntt/Sigma+24-105mm+Art/N/0/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Sigma 24-105mm F/4 DG OS HSM Art Lens
                      </a>
                      is also a good value alternative, but I still like the
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Original_equipment_manufacturer" target="_blank">
                       OEM
                      </a>
                      lenses listed above better.
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      Kids in Action, Short Range Sports, Portraits &amp; More
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      Once you own one of these lenses youll do everything you can to keep it on your camera! New owners often beam with joy when
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chimping" target="_blank">
                       chimping
                      </a>
                      at the rear LCD after taking a shot with these phenomenal lenses, but it comes at a steep price. Most pros will swear by these lenses, so yeah they are worth it  but they are painfully expensive.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      <font color="#ff8000">
                       <strong>
                        I recommend everyone own one of these after they get their all purpose lens.
                       </strong>
                      </font>
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Yes, these lenses are expensive but they are the investment that will allow you to start getting the killer shots youve always wanted. For many the shots you get with them are what you were seeking when you bought your DSLR. The f/2.8 versions below are the fastest focusing and offer the best image stabilization of each of their respective platforms. However they come at not only a great price, but great weight. The f/4 versions also offer wonderful color and super sharp images at a much lighter weight, but they have drawbacks that Ill discuss below. Youll never regret owning one of these lenses  after you get over the sticker shock of paying for them!
                     </p>
                     <table border="0" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0">
                      <tbody>
                       <tr>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/680103-USA/Canon_2751B002_EF_70_200mm_f_2_8L_IS.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                          <img border="0" height="250" src="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/images/images345x345/680103.jpg" style="border-left-width: 0px; border-right-width: 0px; border-bottom-width: 0px; border-top-width: 0px" width="250"/>

                      </tbody>
                     </table>
                     <p>
                      If you can handle the weight, the f/2.8 is the way to go thanks to the dreamy
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bokeh" target="_blank">
                       bokeh
                      </a>
                      they offer  especially when zoomed out to 200mm at f/2.8. The the f/4 versions are similar in sharpness and much lighter weight, so they are a good option but you do sacrifice some bokeh quality. If you photograph people or sports, the f/2.8 lens will allow for greater shutter speeds and the better bokeh allows you to better isolate your subject from background distractions.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      The
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2011/02/b-review-canon-ef-70-300mm-do-f45-56-do.html" target="_blank">
                       Canon EF 70-300mm f/4-5.6L IS USM
                      </a>
                      is an excellent lens but youll trade depth of field for the extra reach. Most people will find that he f/4 lenses are the way to go if they want to step down from the 2.8s for weight or price considerations. I do not recommend the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/449088-USA/Nikon_2161_AF_S_VR_Zoom_Nikkor.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Nikon AF-S VR Zoom-NIKKOR 70-300mm f/4.5-5.6G IF-ED
                      </a>
                      as its image quality has left me underwhelmed.
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      Wide Angle
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      Wide angle lenses are super handy indoors as well as doing landscape work, but in the right hands they can even be used for portrait work in both
                      <a href="http://ronmartinsen.blogspot.com/2009/07/dont-be-afraid-to-share-your-snapshots.html" target="_blank">
                       a traditional way
                      </a>
                      or
                      <a href="http://ronmartinsen.blogspot.com/2010/08/have-fun-with-distortion.html" target="_blank">
                       you can use the distortion they create
                      </a>
                      at an angle to your advantage. They can also be used for a
                      <a href="http://ronmartinsen.blogspot.com/2009/05/going-extra-step-to-make-photo.html" target="_blank">
                       combination of both landscapes or people shots with the landscape as the background
                      </a>
                      .
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      These are really fun lenses to use and have a ton of useful applications!
                     </p>
                     <table border="0" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0">
                      <tbody>
                       <tr>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">

                          <br/>

                           </em>

                     <p>
                      Until the release of the
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2015/03/review-canon-ef-11-24mm-f4l-usm-lensone.html" target="_blank">
                       Canon 11-24mm
                      </a>
                      , the Nikon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/520635-USA/Nikon_2163_AF_S_Zoom_Nikkor_14_24mm.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       14-24mm
                      </a>
                      was considered by many pros to be one of the sharpest lenses on the market. The new Canon 11-24mm leapfrogged it with better image quality and flare control, but for an obscene price. The
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2015/03/review-canon-ef-11-24mm-f4l-usm-lensone.html" target="_blank">
                       11-24mm
                      </a>
                      was so good that my wife  for only the third time in my photography career  told me that I must buy it. I did, and my only regrets were the money I spent and the weight of this lens, but it was worth it.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      For those who might be wondering, I did do a
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2015/04/comparison-tamron-sp-15-30mm-f28-di-vc.html">
                       Tamron SP 15-30mm f/2.8 Di VC USD vs Canon EF 11-24mm f/4L
                      </a>
                      comparison. Ill let you read the results, but lets just say you get what you pay for.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Canon does make the wildly popular
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/279582-USA/Canon_8806A002_17_40mm_f_4L_USM_Lens.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       17-40mm
                      </a>
                      and
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2014/07/comparison-canon-16-35mm-f4l-is-vs-16.html" target="_blank">
                       16-35mm
                      </a>
                      f/2.8L II that I recommended for years, but they are soft and dated compared to their new replacements listed above. As a result, I only recommend them for bargain hunters and typically used is the way to go for these now.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      If you need a super-wide angle I really enjoyed my Canon
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2011/08/mini-review-canon-ef-8-15mm-f4l-fisheye.html" target="_blank">
                       8-15mm
                      </a>
                      , but Nikon has no equivalent lens. Keep in mind though that this is a specialty lens that you wont use very often so dont base your camera purchase decision based on this lens. It also has some serious chromatic aberrations at 8mm and limitations below 10mm.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Some may also suggest the Canon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/351542-USA/Canon_9518A002_EF_S_10_22mm_f_3_5_4_5_USM.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       EF-S 10-22mm f/3.5-4.5 USM
                      </a>
                      (which I loved on my Rebel) or either the Nikon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/277227-USA/Nikon_2144_12_24mm_f_4G_ED_IF_DX.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       AF 12-24mm f/4G ED-IF AF-S DX
                      </a>
                      or Nikon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/619515-REG/Nikon_2181_10_24mm_f_3_5_4_5G_ED_AF_S.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       10-24mm
                      </a>
                      (all for
                      <font color="#ffff00">
                       CROPPPED SENSORS ONLY)
                      </font>
                      .
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      Portrait Lenses  Bokeh Junkies Dream Lenses
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      Warning: These lenses can require longer minimum focus distances and have very slow auto-focus performance so they are best suited for static or slow moving objects. They have a wonderful
                      <a href="http://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bokeh" target="_blank">
                       bokeh
                      </a>
                      though and are all super sharp.
                     </p>
                     <table border="0" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0">
                      <tbody>
                       <tr>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">

                         </a>
                        </td>

                         </a>

                     <p>
                      You can actually save a ton of money and get great results with the much cheaper Canon
                      <a class="desc" cmimpressionsent="1" href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/12140-USA/Canon_2515A003_50mm_f_1_4_USM_Autofocus.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" name="desc">
                       EF 50mm f/1.4 USM
                      </a>
                      or Nikon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/766516-USA/Nikon_2199_AF_S_Nikkor_50mm_f_1_8G.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       50mm f/1.8G
                      </a>
                      . The more expensive lenses are better, but if you arent in need of the best bokeh possible then youll be plenty satisfied with the cheaper options (which is what I did).
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      In 2014 SIGMA finally got my attention by creating a 50mm lens that quite honestly destroys all of the lenses above in terms of sharpness. While I still prefer the 85mm focal length over 50mm for portraits, this is a lens that deserves your serious consideration:
                     </p>
                     <p align="center">
                      <img alt="Sigma 50mm f/1.4 DG HSM" border="0" src="http://static.bhphoto.com/images/multiple_images/images500x500/IMG_385807.jpg" title="Sigma 50mm f/1.4 DG HSM"/>
                      <br/>
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/buy/SIGMA/Ntt/Sigma+50mm+art/N/0/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       <em>
                        Sigma 50mm f/1.4 DG HSM
                       </em>
                      </a>
                      <em>
                       Art Series (
                      </em>
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2014/05/review-sigma-50mm-f14-art-seriesis-it.html" target="_blank">
                       <em>
                        review
                       </em>
                      </a>
                      <em>
                       )
                      </em>
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      I hated SIGMA lenses for years, but the new Art series primes that Ive tested have blown me away. We are talking almost Zeiss Otus good, so I highly recommend their primes. Their zooms havent impressed me yet, but I suspect they will eventually have one that gets my attention.
                      <br/>
                      <br/>
                      Personally, I prefer to shoot my portraits at 200mm using my 70-200mm zoom, but what I lose with that is the very shallow depth of field that can be appealing when you go from f/2.8 to f/1.8 or less. Wedding photographers especially enjoy shooting with these very shallow depth of field lenses, so it all depends on what you need.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      The
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/542292-REG/Canon_2297B002_Telephoto_EF_200mm_f_2L.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Canon EF 200mm f/2L IS USM Lens
                      </a>
                      is also one of my all-time favorite lenses for portraits and offers the best bokeh of any lens Ive tested. Nikon users swear by the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/734998-USA/Nikon_2188_AF_S_NIKKOR_200mm_f_2_0.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Nikon AF-S NIKKOR 200mm f/2G ED VR II Telephoto Lens
                      </a>
                      , so you cant go wrong with either. The downside is that they are super expensive, and very heavy so you really need to be a successful pro to justify the price. Personally I wouldnt buy a 200mm f/2.8 prime them until youve got a good collection of zooms and other lenses first.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Another great option for less money are the amazing Canon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/112539-USA/Canon_2520A004_Telephoto_EF_135mm_f_2_0L.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       EF 135mm f/2.0L USM
                      </a>
                      or Nikon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/113487-USA/Nikon_1935_Telephoto_AF_DC_Nikkor.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       AF DC Nikkor 135mm f/2.0D
                      </a>
                       both of which give you equally wonderful bokeh for a fraction of the cost, but at the expense of poor AF performance and terrible minimum focus distances.
                     </p>
                     <ul>
                      <!--EndFragment-->
                     </ul>
                     <p>
                      Sharpness-wise, you will find that the latest top of the line zoom lenses from Canon and Nikon are just as sharp, if not sharper, than these lenses. Dont be fooled into thinking that you need prime lenses for maximum sharpness because that simply isnt the case (see my
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2015/03/review-canon-ef-11-24mm-f4l-usm-lensone.html" target="_blank">
                       11-24mm
                      </a>
                      review for proof). The lenses do offer better bokeh over the zooms, but thats it. The price you pay for that is a terrible minimum focus distance and very bad focus performance compared to the newest zooms (excluding the SIGMA Art primes).
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      If your pockets are very deep and you dont mind manually focusing, the sharpest lens Ive ever tested are the Zeiss Otus lenses shown below. Its an amazing lens that works for both Canon and Nikon (be sure to buy the right one) but its a specialty lens for scenarios where you can afford to manually focus. The aforementioned SIGMA Art series is almost as good and its autofocus means that most will get sharper shots for a fraction of the price.
                     </p>
                     <p align="center">
                      <img alt="Zeiss 55mm f/1.4 Otus Distagon T* Lens for Canon EF Mount" border="0" src="http://static.bhphoto.com/images/multiple_images/images500x500/IMG_341148.jpg" title="Zeiss 55mm f/1.4 Otus Distagon T* Lens for Canon EF Mount"/>
                      <br/>
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2014/01/mini-review-zeiss-otus-55mm-f14is-it.html" target="_blank">
                       <em>
                        Zeiss 55mm f/1.4 Otus Distagon T* Lens
                       </em>
                      </a>
                      <em>
                       (
                      </em>
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2014/01/mini-review-zeiss-otus-55mm-f14is-it.html" target="_blank">
                       <em>
                        review
                       </em>
                      </a>
                      <em>
                       )
                      </em>
                     </p>
                     <p align="center">
                      <img alt="Zeiss Otus 85mm f/1.4 Apo Planar T* ZE Lens" border="0" src="http://static.bhphoto.com/images/images500x500/1077281.jpg" title="Zeiss Otus 85mm f/1.4 Apo Planar T* ZE Lens"/>
                      <br/>
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/buy/Zeiss/Ntt/otus+85mm/N/0/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Zeiss Otus 85mm f/1.4 Apo Planar T* ZE Lens
                      </a>
                      <em>
                      </em>
                      <em>
                       (
                      </em>
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2014/10/review-zeiss-otus-85mm-f14.html" target="_blank">
                       <em>
                        review
                       </em>
                      </a>
                      <em>
                       )
                      </em>
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      Long Range Sports &amp; Wildlife/Bird Zoom Lenses
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      Theres a joke in the wildlife photography community that the lens you need is always 2x longer the one you have withy, and thats definitely the truth in sports many times as well! These are lenses that most people will be better off renting from
                      <a href="http://ronmartblog.com/2009/11/review-lensrentalscom-lens-rental.html" target="_blank">
                       LensRentals.com
                      </a>
                      rather than spending a fortune on them only to have them collect dust most of the time. However, those who make a living off sports and wildlife shooting will find them a mandatory item in their kit that will pay for themselves. With that, heres my recommendations:
                     </p>
                     <p>
                     </p>
                     <table border="0" cellpadding="2" cellspacing="0">
                      <tbody>
                       <tr>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/732109-USA/Canon_4412B002_EF_400mm_f_2_8L_IS.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                          <img border="0" height="189" src="http://ronmart.smugmug.com/Blog/Reviews/EF-400mm-f28L-IS-II/1000606573_w9vCZ-S-1.jpg" style="border-left-width: 0px; border-right-width: 0px; border-bottom-width: 0px; border-top-width: 0px" width="250"/>
                         </a>
                         <br/>
                         Canon
                         <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/732109-USA/Canon_4412B002_EF_400mm_f_2_8L_IS.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                          EF 400mm f/2.8L IS II USM
                         </a>
                        </td>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <img border="0" height="250" src="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/images/images345x345/520639.jpg" style="border-left-width: 0px; border-right-width: 0px; border-bottom-width: 0px; border-top-width: 0px" width="250"/>
                         <br/>
                         Nikon
                         <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/520639-USA/Nikon_2171_AF_S_Nikkor_400mm_f_2_8G.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787">
                          AF-S Nikkor 400mm f/2.8G ED VR
                         </a>
                        </td>
                       </tr>
                       <tr>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2013/08/review-canon-ef-200-400mm-f4l-is-usm.html" target="_blank">
                          <img border="0" height="250" src="http://static.bhphoto.com/images/images500x500/973129.jpg" style="border-left-width: 0px; border-right-width: 0px; border-bottom-width: 0px; border-top-width: 0px" title="Copyright Ron Martinsen - ALL RIGHTS RESERVED" width="250"/>

                          </em>
                         </a>

                          review
                         </a>

                        </td>
                        <td align="center" valign="middle">
                         <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/688212-USA/Nikon_2187_AF_S_NIKKOR_200_400mm_f_4G.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">

                         </a>
                         <br/>
                         Nikon
                         <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/688212-USA/Nikon_2187_AF_S_NIKKOR_200_400mm_f_4G.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                          AF-S NIKKOR 200-400mm f/4G ED VR II
                         </a>
                        </td>
                       </tr>
                      </tbody>
                     </table>
                     <br/>
                     <p>
                      The new 2.8 versions of the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/732108-USA/Canon_4411B002_EF_300mm_f_2_8L_IS.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Canon 300mm
                      </a>
                      and
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/672200-USA/Nikon_2186_AF_S_NIKKOR_300mm_f_2_8G.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Nikon 300mm
                      </a>
                      are phenomenal lenses that I highly recommend for indoor sports shooters, whereas outdoor the 400mm is significantly more helpful. You can always do extenders, so dont exclude these lenses from your search but my advice is to go with either the 400 prime or the 200-400 first if you are shooting outdoors mostly and can afford it. If you are shooting indoor sports like basketball then the 300mm may be sufficient for your needs.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      To the newcomer, the not so obvious advantage of these big lenses is that you can get a wonderful bokeh but still get enough depth of field at f/2.8 to get an entire subject in focus. Keep this in mind before going up to long lenses that only offer f/5.6 as their wide open aperture.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      For those on a tight budget, the
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2014/01/review-sigma-120-300mm-f28-dg-os-hsm-s.html" target="_blank">
                       SIGMA 120-300mm f/2.8 DG OS HSM | S
                      </a>
                      is pretty sharp, but in my testing it lacked the same focus and stabilization performance of the Canon and Nikon lenses recommended here.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      <strong>
                       Going on a Safari?
                      </strong>
                      The all-new
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2015/02/review-canon-ef-100-400mm-f45-56l-is-ii.html">
                       Canon 100-400m f/4.5-5.6L IS II USM
                      </a>
                      is fantastic and highly recommended for its all purpose functionality if you cant afford to take one of the 200-400mms. While primes are great, most of us only do the Safari once with little control of where we go or the time spent, so having a razor sharp zoom will help increase your odds of getting the shot and avoid having to switch lenses in a dusty environment.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Canon bargain hunters can get decent results with the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/12129-USA/Canon_2526A004_400mm_f_5_6L_USM_Autofocus.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Canon EF 400mm f/5.6L USM Lens
                      </a>
                      , but avoid the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/239654-USA/Canon_7034A002_Telephoto_EF_400mm_f_4_0.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       400mm DO version
                      </a>
                      as its too soft.
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/1081814-REG/canon_8404b002_ef_400mm_f_4_do.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       A new Canon 400mm f/4 DO II
                      </a>
                      is on the way, so it might finally offer the promise of great sharpness and performance in a lighter weight package. However, I havent a a chance to use it yet so Id advise renting or doing research before buying.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Nikon bargain hunters might be tempted to get the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/936121-REG/nikon_80_400mm_f_4_5_5_6g_ed_vr.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Nikon AF-S NIKKOR 80-400mm f/4.5-5.6G ED VR Lens
                      </a>
                      , but when Ive even mentioned it before Ive got numerous emails from readers who said dont waste your money on that lens due to slow focus performance and soft image quality.
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      Sports &amp; Wildlife Super Telephoto Primes
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      These lenses are expensive as some cars, so these are not for the casual shooter. In most cases youll want to rent these rather than owning them simply because they are just too bulky to store and the cost justification just isnt there. I personally recommend renting from
                      <a href="http://ronmartblog.com/2009/11/review-lensrentalscom-lens-rental.html" target="_blank">
                       LensRentals.com
                      </a>
                      and saving your money for other purchases.
                     </p>

                     <p>
                      Yes, they are fun under the right circumstances but these are definitely for experienced pro-level shooters only.
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      Teleconverters
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      Its also quite common for bird hunters to use teleconverters which are lens adapters that multiple the zoom level of your lens by the value of the teleconverter (1.4 times and 2.0 times are most common):
                     </p>
                     <ul>
                      <li>
                       Canon
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/732113-USA/Canon_4409B002_Extender_EF_1_4X_III.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                        1.4x
                       </a>
                       and
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/732111-USA/Canon_4410B002_Extender_EF_2X_III.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                        2x
                       </a>
                      </li>
                      <li>
                       Nikon
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/228165-USA/Nikon_2129_TC_14E_II_1_4x.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                        1.4x
                       </a>
                       and
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/672202-USA/Nikon_2189_AF_S_Teleconverter_TC_20E_III.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                        2x
                       </a>
                      </li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>
                      These sound great, but you can lose auto focus with the 2x on some camera and lens combos, so do your research. You will lose light and sharpness with an extender, but sometimes that tradeoff is worth the extra reach. You should also keep in mind your output format  if you are using a 16 megapixel camera and your highest resolution output will be a 4k television or a 8x12 print then you only need 8 megapixels. This means you can crop 50% of the image and still have high quality without resorting to a 2x teleconverter.
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      Macro
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      Everybody who gets their new DSLR wants to shoot pictures of bugs or flowers so to get the best results you need a macro lens which can be fairly affordable and very useful for more than just macro work. Heres some of my recommendations:
                     </p>
                     <ol>
                      <li>
                       Canon
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/647011-USA/Canon_3554B002_EF_100mm_f_2_8L_Macro.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                        100mm f/2.8L Hybrid IS USM Macro
                       </a>
                       ( or
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/194451-USA/Canon_4657A006_100mm_f_2_8_USM_Macro.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787">
                        EF 100mm f/2.8 USM Macro
                       </a>
                       ) or Nikon
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/424744-USA/Nikon_2160_105mm_f_2_8G_ED_IF_AF_S.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787">
                        AF Micro-Nikkor 105mm f/2.8G ED-IF AF-S VR
                       </a>
                       . Ive used both of the Canons and sharpness-wise my testing showed they were roughly the same, but image stabilization is an advantage worth upgrading to if you can afford it.
                      </li>
                      <li>
                       Canon EF
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/112541-USA/Canon_2539A007_Telephoto_EF_180mm_f_3_5L.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787">
                        180mm f/3.5L Macro USM
                       </a>
                       or Nikon
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/66989-USA/Nikon_1989_Telephoto_AF_Micro_Nikkor.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787">
                        AF Micro Nikkor 200mm f/4.0D ED-IF
                       </a>
                      </li>
                     </ol>
                     <p align="left">
                      TIP: Macro lenses
                      <a href="http://ronmartinsen.blogspot.com/2009/03/capturing-personality.html" target="_blank">
                       make great portrait lenses too
                      </a>
                      ! If you search my
                      <a href="http://ronmartinsen.blogspot.com/" target="_blank">
                       Photographers Notebook
                      </a>
                      youll find plenty of cases where I use my
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/194451-USA/Canon_4657A006_100mm_f_2_8_USM_Macro.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       100mm macro
                      </a>
                      for portraits.
                     </p>
                     <h5>
                      Architecture
                     </h5>
                     <p>
                      The advantage of these lenses is that they feature perspective control which allows you to take an image of a building without distortion.
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2009/02/how-to-use-tilt-shift-lens.html" target="_blank">
                       Heres a really bad article
                      </a>
                      I did a long time ago which demonstrates this point.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Heres some of the recommendations I have:
                     </p>
                     <ol>
                      <li>
                       Canon
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/606804-USA/Canon_3552B002_TS_E_24mm_f_3_5L_II.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                        Tilt Shift TS-E 24mm f/3.5L II Manual Focus
                       </a>
                       or Nikon
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/545664-USA/Nikon_2168_Wide_Angle_PC_E_Nikkor.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787">
                        PC-E Nikkor 24mm f/3.5D ED Manual Focus
                       </a>
                        Outdoors
                      </li>
                      <li>
                       Canon
                       <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/606803-USA/Canon_3553B002_Wide_Tilt_Shift_TS_E_17mm.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                        Tilt Shift TS-E 17mm f/4L
                       </a>
                       (No Nikon equivalent)  Indoors
                      </li>
                     </ol>
                     <p>
                      If you make a living off your tilt-shift work and need the highest possible quality, then Id suggest trying out the
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/buy/Schneider/Ntt/Schneider+PC-TS/N/0/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       Schneider PC-TS
                      </a>
                      series lenses.
                      <br/>
                     </p>
                     <h4>
                      These are just my recommendations
                     </h4>
                     <p>
                      This list is a general guide, not the word of God, so some people will disagree with me. I chose the Canon lenses based on what I would get for each category if I were to (or already have) spent my money. For Nikon, I tried to pick lenses that are equivalent to the Canon's based on discussions with Nikon photographers, but I have less experience with the Nikon lens.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      I have first choice appearing first in the table or indicated by the number 1, a second underneath or indicated by the number 2, etc... In the case where there is no second choice, I personally believe that unless theres a budget or price consideration, youll be happier over the long haul with first lens choice that I recommend. For cases where there are more than two recommended, then you can assume that the third choice is a very good budget choice if your budget is tight. Second choices are usually a compromise over the first choice, but still worth your consideration.
                      <br/>
                      <br/>
                      Another important thing to mention is that entry level
                      <span style="color: #ffff00">
                       Nikon bodies only support autofocus for AF-S and AF-I lenses
                      </span>
                      because these bodies do not contain a built-in focus drive motor. As a result, be careful when shopping so you dont end up with an expensive lens thats manual focus only on your camera body.
                     </p>

                       You should do your own research

                      and use the list above as a starting point. I recommend that you start by reading reviews, and I've found the following sites to be a good starting point:

                       Canon

                        http://www.usa.canon.com/cusa/professional/products/professional_cameras/ef_lens_lineup
                       </a>
                      </li>

                       Nikon
                      </em>

                     <ul>

                       </a>
                      </li>
                      <li>

                      </li>
                     </ul>
                     <p>
                      Once you've narrowed down your list of lenses, you can visit my favorite site to research lenses -
                      <a href="http://www.slrgear.com/reviews/index.php" target="_blank">
                       SLRGear.com
                      </a>
                      and look at their Blur Index for tested lenses (both Nikon and Canon) to see which lens is the sharpest. The Digital Picture also offers a
                      <a href="http://www.the-digital-picture.com/Reviews/ISO-12233-Sample-Crops.aspx?Lens=103&amp;Camera=9&amp;Sample=0&amp;FLI=0&amp;API=3&amp;LensComp=404&amp;CameraComp=9&amp;SampleComp=0&amp;FLIComp=0&amp;APIComp=2" target="_blank">
                       great lens comparison tool
                      </a>
                      .
                      <br/>
                      <br/>
                      Lastly, you can go to places like
                      <a href="http://www.pixel-peeper.com/" target="_blank">
                       http://www.pixel-peeper.com/
                      </a>
                      and Flickr.com to look at actual photos taken with a given lens and body (similar to yours) and see what the final results look like (given the limitations or great skill of the photographer).
                     </p>
                     <h4>
                      <strong>
                       But what about Sigma, Tamron, EF-S, DX lenses, etc...
                      </strong>
                     </h4>
                     <p>
                      Yes, there are alternatives to save you money on lenses, but Ive found that the Canon and Nikon hold their value very well so the resale on the bargain variety third party lenses sucks  big time! People who buy third party lenses eventually get around to buying the high end Canon and Nikon lenses so when they resell them they take a beating. Ive never lost more than 10% when selling my pro lenses but if you search the web youll find the third party lenses going for much less than their Canon and Nikon counterparts. This doesnt mean these lenses arent good, its just an economic reality that Ive observed.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Canon does offer some outstanding EF-S lenses that offer a great value, as does Nikon with some of its DX lenses. However, one consideration for these crop sensor lenses is that you cant take advantage of them on full sensor cameras so if you decide to upgrade you may end up having to sell your lenses at a loss. As a result is my recommendation to get the full frame compatible lenses when possible and if you must get a cropped sensor lens then be sure to get the best ones offered by Canon or Nikon so you can get the best resale price.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      If you do decide to go with third party lenses, Ive noticed a great jump in the quality of SIGMA lenses lately in their new lenses released in 2013 later with the designation of Art, Contemporary and Sports series lenses which can easily identified by the silver dot on the lens (shown below).
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      These new SIGMAs are built at a much higher quality level than I felt the previous models were, so they deserve consideration significantly more than the models that came before them. Heres an example of the first SIGMA lens to blow me away enough to recommend it:
                     </p>
                     <p align="center">
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/buy/Sigma/Ntt/sigma+35mm+dg+hsm/N/0/BI/2071/KBID/2787">
                       <img alt="Sigma 35mm f/1.4 DG HSM Art Series Lens" border="0" src="http://static.bhphoto.com/images/images500x500/898831.jpg" title="Sigma 35mm f/1.4 DG HSM Art Series Lens"/>
                      </a>
                      <br/>
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2013/12/review-sigma-35mm-f14-dg-hsm-art-series.html" target="_blank">
                       <em>
                        Sigma 35mm f/1.4 DG HSM Art Series LensThe Best Sigma Ive Ever Tested
                       </em>
                      </a>
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      As of May 2015, the most recent Art series to blow me away was the 24mm:
                     </p>
                     <p align="center">
                      <img alt="Sigma 24mm f/1.4 DG HSM Art Lens" border="0" src="http://static.bhphoto.com/images/images500x500/1120085.jpg" title="Sigma 24mm f/1.4 DG HSM Art Lens"/>
                      <br/>
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/buy/Sigma/Ntt/Sigma+24mm+Art/N/0/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       <em>
                        Sigma 24mm f/1.4 DG HSM Art Lens
                       </em>
                      </a>
                      <em>
                       (
                      </em>
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2015/04/mini-review-sigma-24mm-f14-dg-hsm-art.html" target="_blank">
                       <em>
                        review
                       </em>
                      </a>
                      <em>
                       )
                      </em>
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      Of all that Ive tested, the aforementioned
                      <a href="http://www.ronmartblog.com/2014/05/review-sigma-50mm-f14-art-seriesis-it.html" target="_blank">
                       SIGMA 50mm
                      </a>
                      Art series is definitely my favorite.
                     </p>
                     <h4>
                      <strong>
                       I've bought my lens, but now I have buyers remorse
                      </strong>
                     </h4>
                     <p>
                      Let's face it, there are so many great lenses that we can afford to purchase so we'll always have to make compromises. This leads to what I call
                      <em>
                       Lens Lust
                      </em>
                      which is a condition whereby you lust after a lens you don't own, but wish you did. For some it is painful, but they survive. For others, it is incurable so they sell their lens (usually at a loss - which they never admit even to themselves) and buy the lens they lust after, only to have the same condition occur again immediately after the purchase. Severe cases of Lens Lust will even cause some to purchase the same lens that they previously owned again at some point in the future as Ive done with the Canon
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/c/product/457680-USA/Canon_1257B002AA_Normal_EF_50mm_f_1_2L.html/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       50mm f/1.2L
                      </a>
                      (which I own now  for the third time).
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      There is a cure for lens lust and it is quite simple - go to forums and read the user reviews of your favorite lenses. On these forums you'll have hundreds of people telling you how awesome your lens is and how it is so much better than other more expensive lenses. Of course, most of these people are writing their reviews shortly after they get their lens, so if you look carefully they'll bash the same lens when they review another lens. This happens because an episode of Lens Lust causes them to replace their short-term beloved lens with something they think is better. In the end, this is easiest way to be happy with your decision. Now stop worrying and go take advantage of
                      <a href="https://www.billmelater.com/about/index.xhtml" target="_blank">
                       BillMeLater by PayPal
                      </a>
                      on
                      <a href="http://www.bhphotovideo.com/bnh/controller/home/BI/2071/KBID/2787" target="_blank">
                       B&amp;H
                      </a>
                      ,
                      <a href="http://amzn.to/1FPadp5" target="_blank">
                       Amazon
                      </a>
                      or
                      <a href="http://www.adorama.com/?kbid=63731" target="_blank">
                       Adorama
                      </a>
                      and get that lens you've been lusting after!
                     </p>
                     <h4>
                      Carrying and Insuring your lenses
                     </h4>
                     <p>
                      Check out my article about
                      <a href="http://ronmartblog.com/2008/06/camera-gear-insurance-don-leave-home.html" target="_blank">
                       camera insurance
                      </a>
                      to find out why you should insure your lenses and why a good bag is so critically important. Insurance costs me about 1% of the cost of my gear  per year, for zero deductible so its a no brainer for me.
                     </p>
                     <p>
                      I highly recommend
                      <a href="http://ronmartblog.com/2010/01/learn-more-about-think-tank-photo.html" target="_blank">
                       Think Tank Photo
                      </a>
                      bags for carrying your lenses as they are bags designed by pro photographers to meet the rough and tumble demands of pro photographers. However, the prices of their bags are competitive with Tamron, Lowepro, etc which I dont find to be nearly as well made. See my reviews for TTP bags at the bottom of
                      <a href="http://ronmartblog.com/2010/01/learn-more-about-think-tank-photo.html" target="_blank">
                       this other article
                      </a>
                      .
                     </p>

                       said...
                      </dt>
                      <dd class="comment-body" id="Blog1_cmt-3625824873442840340">
                       <p>
                        The Canon 70-200 f/2.8L IS II lens is the most amazing value in Canon's lineup. I can handhold at 200mm in as little as 1/15 sec (when I'm stable) and the images are crisp and sharp right out of the camera (can't even do sharpening on some of them). I wouldn't waste your time with the f/4. It's lighter yeah, but it's no match for the 2.8 in my book.
                        <br/>
                        <br/>
                        The 135mm f/2 is fantastic, BUT it's minimum focus distance sucks and the AF is slow. It's a great lens, but the 70-200 f/2.8L II IS USM is just as good (little less bokeh blur though).
                       </p>
                      </dd>

                      <dd class="comment-body" id="Blog1_cmt-7508312870604587765">
                       <p>
                        Hi Paul,
                        <br/>
                        <br/>
                        It depends a lot on your budget and goals. Most short lenses aren't auto focus speed demons. In addition, you can sometimes get better street photography by keeping your distance, so I like using a 70-200 (and Canon makes a great 70-300 - both shown above) to help capture the moment without distracting my subject. The longer lenses are more expensive but they do focus much faster.
                       </p>
                      </dd>

                      <dd class="comment-body" id="Blog1_cmt-5146428532893734909">
                       <p>
                        The AF-D Nikkor 28-105 lens is very good, sharp and has little distortion throughout the zoom range, and also has a decent macro feature. Its a bargain, good ones can be had for $200 or less. Its been my event lens for years.
                        <br/>
                        <br/>
                        Many people don't know that Nikon manual focus AI, AIS and AI-converted lenses can be used with full metering on many DSLRs including D2,3,4,200,600,700,800,7000,7100. Many great MF Nikkors can be found for under $150.
                       </p>
                      </dd>
